  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_94892596-bcba-4850-83b1-8347249ab13c'

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer4897223489796711089">Neste novo romance, o vencedor do prêmio Nobel José Saramago reconta episódios bíblicos do Velho Testamento sob o ponto de vista de Caim, que, depois de assassinar seu irmão, trava um incomum acordo com deus e parte numa jornada que o levará do jardim do Éden aos mais recônditos confins da criação.<br/>Se, em O Evangelho segundo Jesus Cristo, José Saramago nos deu sua visão do </span>
  <span id="freeText4897223489796711089" style="display:none">Neste novo romance, o vencedor do prêmio Nobel José Saramago reconta episódios bíblicos do Velho Testamento sob o ponto de vista de Caim, que, depois de assassinar seu irmão, trava um incomum acordo com deus e parte numa jornada que o levará do jardim do Éden aos mais recônditos confins da criação.<br />Se, em O Evangelho segundo Jesus Cristo, José Saramago nos deu sua visão do Novo Testamento, neste Caim ele se volta aos primeiros livros da Bíblia, do Éden ao dilúvio, imprimindo ao Antigo Testamento a música e o humor refinado que marcam sua obra. Num itinerário heterodoxo, Saramago percorre cidades decadentes e estábulos, palácios de tiranos e campos de batalha, conforme o leitor acompanha uma guerra secular, e de certo modo involuntária, entre criador e criatura. No trajeto, o leitor revisitará episódios bíblicos conhecidos, mas sob uma perspectiva inteiramente diferente.<br />Para atravessar esse caminho árido, um deus às turras com a própria administração colocará Caim, assassino do irmão Abel e primogênito de Adão e Eva, num altivo jegue, e caberá à dupla encontrar o rumo entre as armadilhas do tempo que insistem em atraí-los. A Caim, que leva a marca do senhor na testa e portanto está protegido das iniquidades do homem, resta aceitar o destino amargo e compactuar com o criador, a quem não reserva o melhor dos julgamentos. Tal como o diabo de O Evangelho, o deus que o leitor encontra aqui não é o habitual dos sermões: ao reinventar o Antigo Testamento, Saramago recria também seus principais protagonistas, dando a eles uma roupagem ao mesmo tempo complexa e irônica, cujo tom de farsa da narrativa só faz por acentuar.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4897223489796711089" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2485310454" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2485310454" />
    <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca"><img alt="Jim Fonseca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1397669173p2/6743601.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2485310454?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim Fonseca" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca">Jim Fonseca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780547419893" href="/book/show/10086782-cain">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6743601-jim-fonseca?shelf=religious-theme">religious-theme</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6743601-jim-fonseca?shelf=portuguese-authors">portuguese-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6743601-jim-fonseca?shelf=nobel-prize">nobel-prize</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2485310454" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14804536894391507687">Another great book from the Nobel-prize-winning (1998) Portuguese author. It’s a re-imagining of the Old Testament (which the author has called the Book of Nonsense) in the way that the author’s “The Gospel According to Jesus Christ” re-imagined the New. (In the book, the author does not capitalize proper names but I did so because it looks odd otherwise.) <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1533644767i/26090677._SY540_.jpg' width='263' height='363' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Cain slays his brother, Abel, and then travels through various times and places of the Old Testament. In his wanderings he stumbles across Ab</span>
  <span id="freeText14804536894391507687" style="display:none">Another great book from the Nobel-prize-winning (1998) Portuguese author. It’s a re-imagining of the Old Testament (which the author has called the Book of Nonsense) in the way that the author’s “The Gospel According to Jesus Christ” re-imagined the New. (In the book, the author does not capitalize proper names but I did so because it looks odd otherwise.) <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1533644767i/26090677._SY540_.jpg" width="263" height="363" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Cain slays his brother, Abel, and then travels through various times and places of the Old Testament. In his wanderings he stumbles across Abraham ready to sacrifice his son Isaac. He watches the Tower of Babel being constructed. He’s a laborer for Job and watches his sufferings. Cain works on the construction of Noah’s ark. He sees Lot’s wife turn to salt. He becomes a paramour of Lilith and even has a son by her. Cain watches folks worship the golden calf and he see the walls of Jericho tumble. <br><br>Cain is quite a kibitzer. During his travels he argues with various angels and occasionally with God. Why did you make me slay my brother? I had no choice. Why did you kill all those innocent children when you destroyed Sodom and Gomorrah? Why in the world would you ask Abraham to kill his son? What did Job ever do to you that you made him suffer so?<br><br>Some samples:<br><br>“But why put to the test the very thing you yourself created? <br><br>“Poor me, said Eve, what a bad beginning, and what a sad fate will be mine.” <br><br>“You gods should still take the blame for all the crimes committed in your name or because of you…”<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1533645028i/26090689._SX540_.jpg" width="363" height="242" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>“…the lord is not a person to be trusted.”<br><br>“It is quite possible that the pact that some say exists between god and men contains only two articles, namely, you scratch my back and I’ll scratch yours.”<br><br>“…but I have learned one thing…that our god, the creator of heaven and earth, is completely mad…”<br><br>“…the lord isn’t listening, he’s deaf, everywhere the poor, unfortunate and wretched cry out to him for help…and the lord turns his back on them…”<br><br>“it seems likely that satan is just another instrument of the lord, the one who does the dirty work to which god prefers not to put his name.” <br><br>“The history of mankind is the history of our misunderstandings with god, for he doesn’t understand us, and we don’t understand him.”<br><br>Original, thought-provoking, and a quick read.<br><br>Top image: Lot's Wife by Shawna Atkins from shawnaatkinsart.blogspot.com<br>Bottom image: Walls of Jericho from gettyimages.com <br></span>
  <a data-text-id="14804536894391507687" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12641060002529837789">The God of the New Testament is a much more murderous god than that of the Old. In the Old, Abraham is asked to sacrifice Isaac, but God stops him from doing so. In the New, God has Jesus killed in a slow, painful, tortuous death. What kind of god thinks a blood sacrifice to the death makes up for anything? <br/><br/>I listened to this book rather than read it because I can't stand Saramago's idiosyncratic punctuation (or lack of it, more like). But the voice of both the narrator and the author, the smarm</span>
  <span id="freeText12641060002529837789" style="display:none">The God of the New Testament is a much more murderous god than that of the Old. In the Old, Abraham is asked to sacrifice Isaac, but God stops him from doing so. In the New, God has Jesus killed in a slow, painful, tortuous death. What kind of god thinks a blood sacrifice to the death makes up for anything? <br /><br />I listened to this book rather than read it because I can't stand Saramago's idiosyncratic punctuation (or lack of it, more like). But the voice of both the narrator and the author, the smarminess, the all-knowingness (which was part of the book) just irritated me. I have loved almost all Saramago books I've read, but not this one. I got within spitting distance of the end, but dnf'd it, just had enough and so I'm out of step with almost everyone else who loved the book.</span>

<div id="review_1528348476" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1528348476" />
    <a title="Agnieszka" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2446508-agnieszka"><img alt="Agnieszka" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1371835955p2/2446508.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Agnieszka" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Agnieszka" href="/user/show/2446508-agnieszka">Agnieszka</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19173431-cain">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=saramago">saramago</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1528348476" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7459995451298444326"><br/>The lord, also known as god, created adam and eve, but due to tiredness of the act of creation or perhaps other minor distractions forgot to bestow them with the gift of speech, so to remedy that just stuck unceremoniously his tongue into them. However it seemed bizarre it worked excessively well but what adam did with his new art, <i>Let’s go to bed</i>, he said to eve. Typical. Ok, ok he probably couldn’t say how lovely dressed you are since they were naked, but still, though they hadn’t bed too, I g</span>
  <span id="freeText7459995451298444326" style="display:none"><br />The lord, also known as god, created adam and eve, but due to tiredness of the act of creation or perhaps other minor distractions forgot to bestow them with the gift of speech, so to remedy that just stuck unceremoniously his tongue into them. However it seemed bizarre it worked excessively well but what adam did with his new art, <i>Let’s go to bed</i>, he said to eve. Typical. Ok, ok he probably couldn’t say how lovely dressed you are since they were naked, but still, though they hadn’t bed too, I guess. Anyway, they went to bed and had children: cain, abel and set, though about the latter we mention only from chronicler duty since he is neither here nor there in that story.<br /><br />And Cain killed Abel what is commonly known even to these one not familiar with Genesis and what is not known is the way Saramago retold the story of hapless Cain, the first assassin in world history. According to Saramago Cain was condemned to wander for ever about the world and due to that could participate in stories described in Old Testament and see with own eyes burning of Sodoma and Gomora, for what he did hate the Lord wholeheartedly, destroying the Babel Tower, sacrificing Isaac by Abraham or trials of Job to finish his journey on the Noah's Ark. And all the way he would challenge and undermine God's authority and his decisions.<br /><br /><i>The history of mankind is the history of our misunderstandings with god, for he doesn’t understand us, and we don’t understand him</i>. <br /><br />Novel, written in typical for Saramago style with long digressive sentences without punctuation and unconventional approach to the subject is witty, wonderfully quizzical, filled with blasphemous statements. And though that looks like some light reading nonetheless it leaves us with some uncomfortable questions and unwanted truths. It challenges us to consider nature of good and evil, free will, morality, determinism and responsibility for own deeds. God from Old Testament is neither kind-hearted nor forgiving old fellow. He is demanding and punishing, vengeful and unjust. Given the terrible lot of Sodoma and Gomora, burnt to the ground, did Lord find women guilty too, and if so, what about the children, Cain would ask. <br /><br /><i>Your mistake is to assume that guilt is understood in the same way by god and by men</i>.<br /><br />If God is that omniscient how is it even possible the world is as it is, we could ask. Are we only a plaything in his hands, a wager in his neverending struggle with Satan ?<br /><br /><i>the innocent are now accustomed to paying for the sinners, The lord seems to have a very strange idea of justice</i>.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7459995451298444326" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1770095922" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1770095922" />
    <a title="BlackOxford" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford"><img alt="BlackOxford" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484863606p2/17744555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1770095922?book_show_action=true">Sep 28, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="BlackOxford" class="user" itemprop="url" name="BlackOxford" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford">BlackOxford</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9786071103161" href="/book/show/7086348-ca-n">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=portuguese-language">portuguese-language</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1770095922" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4635365196766410379"><b>A Portuguese Midrash</b><br/><br/>Saramago’s Cain is a traditional Midrash, a meditative, speculative commentary on the Torah, the first five books of the Jewish and Christian bibles. Despite its often comedic, mostly polemic assessment of the God of the Torah, conventionally ascribed to Moses, Saramago isn’t the first or the most strident critic to take seriously the personality of Yahweh and what it might imply for humanity and the rest of creation. <br/><br/>The most remarkable aspect of the work isn’t its content b</span>
  <span id="freeText4635365196766410379" style="display:none"><b>A Portuguese Midrash</b><br><br>Saramago’s Cain is a traditional Midrash, a meditative, speculative commentary on the Torah, the first five books of the Jewish and Christian bibles. Despite its often comedic, mostly polemic assessment of the God of the Torah, conventionally ascribed to Moses, Saramago isn’t the first or the most strident critic to take seriously the personality of Yahweh and what it might imply for humanity and the rest of creation. <br><br>The most remarkable aspect of the work isn’t its content but its use of a time-travelling Cain who can peek in on various biblical episodes from Abraham’s preparation to murder his son Isaac, to the annihilation of the residents, children included, of Sodom and Gomorrah, to Joshua’s divinely sanctioned genocide of the Midianites and their neighbours, to the pointless torture of Job, to all of the gore and dispossession in between. Cain therefore has a sort of synoptic view, to anticipate the New Testament term, from which he reports the violence, irritability, irrationality, ruthlessness, injustice and simply arbitrary imposition of misery on the world by the Lord.<br><br>The character of the God of the Torah has been problematic since at least the time of Christ. For Christians, because the intellectual and moral chasm between the perpetually vengeful Yahweh and the perpetually loving Father of Jesus appears to be unbridgeable. For Jews, because by the time of Christ the rabbinate was preaching a sermon of love and forgiveness almost indistinguishable from the gospels of Jesus. <br><br>From time to time, theologians have attempted to square this divine circle by claiming mitigating circumstances - the distinctly illiberal caste of ancient cultures, for example, or the possible inability of human minds to deal with the full monty of revelation all at once. None of these rationalisations work out terribly well, mostly they just open the way for a free for all interpretation of ‘inerrant’ scripture and further heresy; or they promote the equally worrisome idea of ‘continuing revelation’ which neither dogmatic Jews or Christians - with the exception of the Mormons - can tolerate.<br><br>The second century heretic Marcion took some understandable action - not long after the publication of the love-imbued (and anti-semitic) Gospel of John - by simply rejecting the Jewish scriptures in their entirety. The Protestant reformer, John Calvin, took the opposite tack and preached the justified vengeance of a wilful and arbitrary God on a sin-infected, unworthy creation. <br><br>The only time the controversy subsides is when believers simply choose to ignore the all too obvious contradictions. Catholics do this as a matter of principle, accepting both divine love and vengeance as a ‘mystery’, thus putting the problem beyond recognition much less discussion. Liberal rabbis generally grab for the nearest shibboleth on the inscrutability of the divine mind.<br><br>And this equivocation is something Saramago will not tolerate. The God of the Torah is clearly insane. This would be only a dogmatic issue of sectarian importance if it weren’t for the fact that this God has been a perennial role model of leadership from Joshua to Donald Trump - you’re with me or against me; there is no qualified loyalty; nothing stands in the way of achieving one’s personal objectives; ruthless persistence at any cost is a virtue, etc. This is a God who justifies violence and deceit and patent injustice as a matter of divine right. Cain summarises the situation:<br><blockquote>“Burning sodom and gomorrah to the ground had evidently not been enough for the lord, for here, at the foot of mt sinai, was clear, irrefutable proof of his wickedness, three thousand men killed simply because he was angered by the creation of a supposed rival in the form of a golden calf. I killed one brother and the lord punished me, who, I would like to know, is going to punish the lord for all these deaths, thought cain, lucifer was quite right when he rebelled against god, and those who say he did so out of envy are wrong, he simply recognised god's evil nature.”</blockquote><br>This is the God of War and very little else. Saramago wants us to remember this.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1475875834i/20777927._SY540_.png" width="400" height="700" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="4635365196766410379" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Fabian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian"><img alt="Fabian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514061293p2/1036893.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1161303732?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fabian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fabian" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian">Fabian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780547419893" href="/book/show/10086782-cain">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1161303732" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17309352108830755998">I get Saramago's genius finally. Way late*, yeah, but I FINALLY get it...!!<br/><br/>(cue to me running barefoot downhill, waving arms, surrendering to literary genius at long last)<br/><br/>Sacrilegious, funny--the prose finally twines itself with my alert reader's sensitivity... the cascade of commas, the deficit of paragraphs, capitalizations, periods: all the magician's tricks finally seem to me to be EARNED. Previously, I've been uninspired by "The History of the Siege of Lisbon" and "The Double": the acrobati</span>
  <span id="freeText17309352108830755998" style="display:none">I get Saramago's genius finally. Way late*, yeah, but I FINALLY get it...!!<br /><br />(cue to me running barefoot downhill, waving arms, surrendering to literary genius at long last)<br /><br />Sacrilegious, funny--the prose finally twines itself with my alert reader's sensitivity... the cascade of commas, the deficit of paragraphs, capitalizations, periods: all the magician's tricks finally seem to me to be EARNED. Previously, I've been uninspired by "The History of the Siege of Lisbon" and "The Double": the acrobatics leading to nothing but small stories with tiny miracles in 'em. But, at last, the subject seems to fit the writer's own predilections: of making long streaming sentences (a trick of Garcia Marquez as well) build entire tableaus in the roving mind.<br /><br />And the tableaus here range from ghastly to heavenly, as god and satan himself (notice the lack of capitalization, as if everything, EVERYTHING biblical has a shadiness of apocrypha) battle it out. This one reminds me of "Memnoch the Devil" by Anne Rice (indeed, her newer "Angel Time" also deals with "alternative presents": the various adventures that Cain, not the killer of society but the independent hero, experiences). Finally, the hokeyness of Saramago (a.k.a. time travel of biblical figures) is minimized to more accessible levels. Ironically, writing about these grand themes finally becomes his most personal &amp; worthwhile work.<br /><br />*CAIN's his final novel</span>
  <a data-text-id="17309352108830755998" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1344064006" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1344064006" />
    <a title="Jibran" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5407952-jibran"><img alt="Jibran" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572721231p2/5407952.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1344064006?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jibran" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jibran" href="/user/show/5407952-jibran">Jibran</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781846555435" href="/book/show/11734160-cain">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jibran by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/16077597-matt">Matt</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5407952-jibran?shelf=god">god</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5407952-jibran?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5407952-jibran?shelf=nobel">nobel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1344064006" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2495540877900307726"><i>The life of a god isn’t as easy as you think,</i> said God to Cain.<br/><br/>When Cain meets an angel and enters into an argument about the oblation god has ordered Abraham to make of his own son, the angel, feeling trapped, mutters under his breath, <i>Oh no, a rationalist,</i> and tries to sneak away to complete the divine mission with which he’s charged. The angel might have turned a deaf ear to Cain’s cold logic, but you, the angelic reader, have no recourse but to subject yourself to his grilling.<br/><br/>The hero or an</span>
  <span id="freeText2495540877900307726" style="display:none"><i>The life of a god isn’t as easy as you think,</i> said God to Cain.<br /><br />When Cain meets an angel and enters into an argument about the oblation god has ordered Abraham to make of his own son, the angel, feeling trapped, mutters under his breath, <i>Oh no, a rationalist,</i> and tries to sneak away to complete the divine mission with which he’s charged. The angel might have turned a deaf ear to Cain’s cold logic, but you, the angelic reader, have no recourse but to subject yourself to his grilling.<br /><br />The hero or anti-hero of this story, depending on one’s point of view, is none but Cain, Adam’s other son, the first murderer, the embodiment of evil, transformed for the purpose into the author’s rationalist alter ego, shredding to bits the moral fabric Biblical history is made of, with the confidence of a devil and the precision of an infallible angel. <br /><br />Saramago’s purpose here is not to tell us something new; we have heard all that before. Nor is there an attempt to engage the rationalist and the religionist on a profounder philosophical level. Instead, he takes to task the sacred and the revered through the felicitous agency of satire, just that this time it is caustic, hilarious, direct, with no attempt at subtlety, with a delicious tongue-smacking of an irreverent blasphemer, the title he earned for writing this story. In any case, it’s guaranteed to amuse you or enrage you, depending, again, on your point of view!<br /><br />As I read on I couldn’t help recalling Voltaire’s <i>Candide</i> in which chance compels a young and intelligent man to traverse the earth in search of some universal dicta that would help him answer questions about life. Likewise our protagonist Cain, carrying a curse by god as punishment for unjustly killing his brother, is condemned to travel between places and ages, and like Machado de Assis’ <i>Brás Cubas,</i> he goes beyond the confines of human time to meet various Biblical characters and sees the history, as crystallised in the Old Testament, unfold before his sinful eyes. <br /><br />Be it Moses’ genocidal campaigns on god’s behest or the gambler’s wager between god and satan for Job’s test of patience, whether it’s the total annihilation of Sodom &amp; Gomorrah for – guess what – homosexuality or the essentially immoral tale of god, like an insane ruler, ordering Abraham to put to sword his own son as a “test” of his faith, the more Cain goes around the more evidence he sees of god’s wickedness and irrationality, amazed at the games he plays with humans he created to prove his superiority and uniqueness, perhaps in a bawdy attempt to escape his own ennui or to placate his inflated divine ego. On the last stop in Cain’s journey before the curtain falls he meets Noah and hears of god’s grand plan to destroy all of humanity except Noah’s family and those who manage to clamber upon the ark. Turning over in his head the mountain of evidence of god’s evil ways Cain tells Noah:<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“I have learned one thing, What’s that, That our god, the creator of heaven and earth, is completely mad, …innocent are now accustomed to paying for the sinners, the lord seems to have a very strange idea of justice, the idea of someone who hasn’t the slightest notion of what human justice might be.”</i>
</blockquote><br />All in all, there are lots of chuckles and gasps to be had here, in signature Saramago style. <br /><br /><br />August 2015</span>
  <a data-text-id="2495540877900307726" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

We jump forwards and jump backwards in the genesis narrative, and every time there is a change in gear the reader is given a jolt. And a new breath and freshness sweeps over this reverted genesis.<br/><br/>Saramago’s Cain is a disappointed man. And it is his disappoin</span>
  <span id="freeText5163798539825504856" style="display:none"><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1450109318i/17378686.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><blockquote>

We jump forwards and jump backwards in the genesis narrative, and every time there is a change in gear the reader is given a jolt. And a new breath and freshness sweeps over this reverted genesis.<br><br>Saramago’s Cain is a disappointed man. And it is his disappointment that has created all his troubles. Granted, there was jealousy in the deed for which he is known. But at the root of the jealousy there was a drive towards god. And there lies the disappointment. The story then becomes a quixotic quest (for there is always an explicit quixotic element in Saramago’s characters – part of Saramago’s mark) as Cain searches through the holy book for the presence of the love and justice that he expects out of his god. And the search turns out to be quixotic indeed.<br><br>Saramago’s mark can also be felt in one’s ribs (not the one from which Cain’s mother originated), but because humour and laughter always spring out of his writing.<br><br></span>

<div id="review_475178041" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/475178041" />
    <a title="Fionnuala" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5498525-fionnuala"><img alt="Fionnuala" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368182051p2/5498525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/475178041?book_show_action=true">Dec 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fionnuala" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fionnuala" href="/user/show/5498525-fionnuala">Fionnuala</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781846555435" href="/book/show/11734160-cain">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=review-may-contain-comic-content">review-may-contain-comic-content</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=satire">satire</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=saramago-and-related">saramago-and-related</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer475178041" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10137479475522978505"><br/>José Saramago is certainly not the first writer to question the logic of the old testament, the legitimacy of the Lord's pronouncements, and the merits of the prophets who interpreted those pronouncements for the benefit of the descendants of Adam and Eve, and sometime later, for the benefit of descendants of the very small gene pool of Noah’s family who survived the Flood and repopulated the earth when everyone else was drowned, but Saramago is possibly one of the most creative and original to </span>
  <span id="freeText10137479475522978505" style="display:none"><br />José Saramago is certainly not the first writer to question the logic of the old testament, the legitimacy of the Lord's pronouncements, and the merits of the prophets who interpreted those pronouncements for the benefit of the descendants of Adam and Eve, and sometime later, for the benefit of descendants of the very small gene pool of Noah’s family who survived the Flood and repopulated the earth when everyone else was drowned, but Saramago is possibly one of the most creative and original to treat the subject in his very distinctive style which, in this case, is very averse to the use of conventions like the full stop or quotation marks as if they might have been ordained for our use by the Lord and therefore may not be used in this piece of writing which, after all, is a challenge to all the laws laid down to regulate the lives of the chosen people, but oddly enough, he, Saramago, that is, and not the Lord, has retained the use of the humble comma which he uses well and often so that once we have accepted this, the narrative of Cain’s wanderings over the ancient world and across the centuries of the Old Testament, interfering here and there with the Lord’s decisions while also keeping an eye on the Devil’s activities and generally applying Saramago’s brand of no-nonsense reasoning to the various episodes he encounters in his peregrinations, all of which are entertaining in an odd way even while they underline the shaky foundations of Old Testament wisdom, leads the reader entertainingly and finally to a full stop.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10137479475522978505" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_399106089" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/399106089" />
    <a title="Kinga" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/110812-kinga"><img alt="Kinga" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1491308194p2/110812.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/399106089?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kinga" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kinga" href="/user/show/110812-kinga">Kinga</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099552246" href="/book/show/13170570-cain">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/110812-kinga?shelf=pub-2009">pub-2009</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer399106089" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10983921641007949895">In 'Cain', published just a few months before Saramago’s death, the author gets to argue with God one last time. On this occasion his jibes are aimed at the god of the Old Testament, who, as we all know, is an easy target. This god is cruel, proud, jealous, vindictive, inconsistent and often simply petty.<br/><br/>Saramago hires Cain to call out god on all his sins and errors beginning with Cain’s own sorry story which made him go down in history as the first murderer of the worst kind. But weren’t there </span>
  <span id="freeText10983921641007949895" style="display:none">In 'Cain', published just a few months before Saramago’s death, the author gets to argue with God one last time. On this occasion his jibes are aimed at the god of the Old Testament, who, as we all know, is an easy target. This god is cruel, proud, jealous, vindictive, inconsistent and often simply petty.<br /><br />Saramago hires Cain to call out god on all his sins and errors beginning with Cain’s own sorry story which made him go down in history as the first murderer of the worst kind. But weren’t there any extenuations? After all, it’s god who started it. He was the one that created that unhealthy sibling rivalry between Abel and Cain by asking them for offerings and judging them. Abel, who took care of livestock brought a nice juicy lamb, and Cain who was a farmer brought god veggies. Sadly, god was not a vegetarian so he snubbed Cain’s offering each and every time. OK, so Cain overreacted. Murder is never the answer. But maybe god should take some of the responsibility, too?<br /><br />This is the question that is being asked many times throughout this little book. Cain, condemned to endless wandering, appears whenever and wherever god’s actions are questionable. What sort of god asks someone to kill their beloved son to prove some stupid point? And isn’t killing against god’s very own commandments? Saramago reveals that it was actually Cain himself who stopped Abraham from killing Isaac as the angel sent on that mission was running late. Back when I was a good church going little girl I remember that story scared me the most. I was surprised it was never mentioned how Isaac felt about the fact that his dad just tried to kill him. Here is how it went according to Saramago:<br /><br />“Isaac asked, Father, whatever did I do to you that would make you want to kill me, your only son, You did nothing wrong, Isaac, So why did you want to cut my throat as if I were a lamb, asked the boy, if that man, may the lord’s blessings be upon him, hadn’t come and grabbed your arm, you would now be carrying home a corpse, It was lord’s idea, he meant it as a test, A test of what, Of my faith and my obedience, What kind of lord would order a father to kill his own son, He’s the only lord we have, the lord of our ancestors, the lord who was here when we were born, And if that lord had a son, would he ordered him to be killed as well, asked Isaac, Time will tell.”<br /><br />And what about the children of Sodom and Gomorrah? Surely they were innocent, weren’t they? And that whole Job business, that really was unnecessarily cruel. If any human were to test anybody’s love that way they would’ve been deemed psychopathic. Not to mention that replacing all the children of Job whom god killed with new children does not actually make it ok.<br /><br />And so it goes, whenever god acts up comes Cain to ask the uncomfortable questions. Sometimes they are big things like just why exactly would god help certain tribes in their battles by killing thousands of people? Sounds rather bloodthirsty. Other times they are just minor inconsistencies – if Lot was so drunk that he passed out and had no idea his daughters were having sex with him, how did he manage to get it up?<br /><br />Throughout the book Cain gets angrier and angrier with god, until he finally finds himself on the Ark with Noah and decides it’s time for revenge which is where the story ends.<br /><br />'Cain' is a funny little allegory but I couldn’t help but be saddened a little by how disappointed Saramago seemed to be with both god and the humankind. Nonetheless, it’s hard to disagree with him even if his jokes seem almost juvenile occasionally. I came to similar conclusions when I was nine and they explained to me that we must worship god all the time, otherwise he would get angry and send us to hell. I thought: what a drama queen!</span>
  <a data-text-id="10983921641007949895" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_453658769" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/453658769" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/453658769?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780547419893" href="/book/show/10086782-cain">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die (2012)
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=nobel">nobel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=1001-new-addition">1001-new-addition</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer453658769" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17394581588495415900">We all know that Cain was the firstborn of Adam and Eve. He was a farmer and he killed Abel, the first human to die, because of jealousy due to later being more favored by God. That made him the first human murderer and he was condemned by God and made him roam the earth forever. This book, the last book completed by Nobel laureate Jose de Suosa Saramago (1922-2010) before his death, <i>Cain</i> is about him and his journey after the killing. Like Forrest Gump, the wandering <b>Cain</b> of Saramago witnessed </span>
  <span id="freeText17394581588495415900" style="display:none">We all know that Cain was the firstborn of Adam and Eve. He was a farmer and he killed Abel, the first human to die, because of jealousy due to later being more favored by God. That made him the first human murderer and he was condemned by God and made him roam the earth forever. This book, the last book completed by Nobel laureate Jose de Suosa Saramago (1922-2010) before his death, <i>Cain</i> is about him and his journey after the killing. Like Forrest Gump, the wandering <b>Cain</b> of Saramago witnessed the important events during his lifetime that seemed to be forever. Well, some people in the Old Testament lived almost a thousand years. Cain here was there during the attempted killing of Isaac by his father Abraham, the trials of Job, Moses finding his people worshiping Baal, the destruction of Sodom and Gomorrah, the Great Flood, etc.<br /><br />Just like Saramago's earlier work <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28859.The_Gospel_According_to_Jesus_Christ" title="The Gospel According to Jesus Christ by José Saramago" rel="nofollow noopener">The Gospel According to Jesus Christ</a> (3 stars), this book takes Saramago's no-holds-barred interpretations of biblical stories that we are all familiar with. Unlike the type of interpretations that are supposed to inspire you to being better human beings, Saramago's works make you think and smile. Think because Saramago pries into the bible-truths that we thought we should not question when we were young because those were how your catechists told you to interpret them. Think because if you apply common sense, probably if you are not a biblical scholar like me, chances are, you would be amazed on how Saramago thought of those spins and twists. Also, his works make you smile because some of his dialogues, particularly those arguments between God and Cain are outright funny because after Cain was cursed by God, Cain felt that he was God's equal and he could say what he wanted to say to Him. The result is like God and Cain arguing endlessly like two eager-beaver college debaters.<br /><br />Highly recommended to all Saramago's fans.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17394581588495415900" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9474829822934137837">Following humanity's (biblical) first murderer in an epic through the places of the Old Testament: what more could you ask? Saramago settles his accounts with a god he considers monstrous, through the mouth of Cain, who serves as an incredulous witness to all the horrors of the god of death he meets. The central thesis: the reasons for the Lord should not be impenetrable.<br/><br/>The dialogues are tasty; the resentment is real; the end stands out as the most perfect of obviousness. Because if Cain killed</span>
  <span id="freeText9474829822934137837" style="display:none">Following humanity's (biblical) first murderer in an epic through the places of the Old Testament: what more could you ask? Saramago settles his accounts with a god he considers monstrous, through the mouth of Cain, who serves as an incredulous witness to all the horrors of the god of death he meets. The central thesis: the reasons for the Lord should not be impenetrable.<br /><br />The dialogues are tasty; the resentment is real; the end stands out as the most perfect of obviousness. Because if Cain killed his brother, it is because he could not kill the god who refused (for no apparent reason) his offerings. But there might be other ways to kill a god.</span>

<div id="review_591935830" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/591935830" />
    <a title="Tony" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33771-tony"><img alt="Tony" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1292869016p2/33771.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/591935830?book_show_action=true">Apr 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tony" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tony" href="/user/show/33771-tony">Tony</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780547419893" href="/book/show/10086782-cain">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33771-tony?shelf=portugese">portugese</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33771-tony?shelf=religion">religion</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer591935830" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6967874118944051285">The Bible is a playground of stories, of characters, of parables, of moral dilemmas. Some take it literally, the WORD OF GOD. Some think it nonsense. Highly entertaining nonsense. But nonsense nonetheless.<br/><br/>Saramago, in this his final work, thinks it nonsense. He has tapped this mine before, in <i>The Gospel According to Jesus Christ</i>, but here he goes again, telling the story of Cain and Abel but then projecting Cain as an observer and sometime participant on other Bible stories. Thus: Cain shows up </span>
  <span id="freeText6967874118944051285" style="display:none">The Bible is a playground of stories, of characters, of parables, of moral dilemmas. Some take it literally, the WORD OF GOD. Some think it nonsense. Highly entertaining nonsense. But nonsense nonetheless.<br /><br />Saramago, in this his final work, thinks it nonsense. He has tapped this mine before, in <i>The Gospel According to Jesus Christ</i>, but here he goes again, telling the story of Cain and Abel but then projecting Cain as an observer and sometime participant on other Bible stories. Thus: Cain shows up and disrupts Abraham’s slaying of his son Isaac, forced to act because the Angel of the Lord got delayed (<i>Abe said, "Man, you must be putting me on!"</i>); he watches innocent children slaughtered at Sodom; he does the Tower of Babel and Moses and the Golden Calf. Cain is there when God and Satan make a bet to see if Job will lose his religion if they destroy his life. (I think John Landis worked this vignette into a movie many years later, when Wall Street magnates Mortimer and Randolph Duke decide to make a similar $1 bet over the life of Louis Winthorpe III). Cain, a wanderer by divine fiat, moseys up on Noah and his sons at their ark building and, learning of the Lord’s intentions of drowning every human being except the saintly Noah clan in order to rid the world of human evil, correctly predicts that God will fare no better in this do-over than he did the first time.<br /><br />Simple enough, right? Saramago makes literary fun of Bible stories, showing that the Biblical god is petulant, vengeful, murderous, unjust, often forgetful and almost humanly fallible. So, believers can humph, and haters gonna hate. <br /><br />But wait.<br /><br />There’s one ‘story’ in here that is not strictly from the Bible. At least not the Bible as I remember it.<br /><br />(Some minor spoilers follow).<br /><br />After Cain murders his brother (because the Lord would not accept Cain’s offerings), the Lord puts a ‘mark’ on Cain’s forehead and sentences him to a life of wandering. He winds up enslaved in the palace of Queen Lilith and her husband Noah. His first job there is mud treader. Which is a pretty shitty job. But Queen Lilith takes a shine to him and makes him her bedchamber slave boytoy. Cain finds this happier work than mudtreading. <br /><br />Saramago sets up this switch in duties beautifully with this passage:<br /><br /><i>Like everything else, words have their whys and wherefores. Some call to us solemnly, arrogantly, giving themselves airs, as if they were destined for great things, and then it turns out that they were nothing more than a breeze too light even to set the sail of a windmill moving, whereas other ordinary, habitual words, the sort you use every day end up having consequences no one would have dared predict, they weren’t born for that and yet they shook the world. The overseer said, Enter.</i><br /><br />Yes, a simple word. Enter. But what are we, the readers, entering. <br /><br />There is no Biblical character named Lilith. In <i>Isaiah</i>, however, in a description of some desolate, chaotic land, we hear, “Wildcats shall meet hyenas, / Goat-demons shall greet each other; / There too the lilith shall repose / And find herself a resting place”. <br /><br />I know. I don’t know either. People with more time on their hands have thought about this though. Medieval Jewish folklorists thus have posited that Lilith is Adam’s <i>first</i> wife. Goethe, Browning, George MacDonald and a whole lot of painters have worked the whole <i>Lilith</i> angle. <br /><br />In an article in <i>Bible History Daily</i>* entitled <i>Lilith: Seductress, Heroine or Murderer</i>, Janet Howe Gaines writes, “Irish novelist James Joyce cast her as the “patron of abortions.” Modern feminists celebrate her bold struggle for independence from Adam. Her name appears as the title of a Jewish women’s magazine and a national literacy program. An annual music festival that donates its profits to battered women’s shelters and breast cancer research institutes is called the Lilith Fair.”<br /><br />Saramago paints her, instead, as a nymphomaniac. <br /><br />I apologize here, because I don’t usually go all <i>Wiki</i> in my reviews. I just couldn’t help myself doing a little research. I got confused because Lilith’s cuckold husband was named Noah, but seemingly a different <i>Noah</i> from the famous shipbuilder. Or the same. Or. <br /><br />________<br /><br />So God speaks:<br /><br /><i>My power is absolute, limitless…the only person I have to account to is myself…I never have to concern myself with considerations of a personal nature…I am endowed , let me say this to you now, with a conscience so flexible that it agrees with whatever I do.</i><br /><br />But Cain sees all this. And he sees the future clearly. We are His image going forward, ya know. And thanks to this re-imagining, Cain is on the Ark. There are just these few folks left. It isn’t like Cain hasn’t killed before. He can end this right now. Save a lot of CNN headlines.<br /><br />God destroyed Sodom, innocent children slaughtered along with the fetishists. I read this this week as innocent children were slaughtered while waiting for their parents to finish a race. It is likely they too are collateral damage in God’s Will.<br /><br /><br />*No, I am not a subscriber.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6967874118944051285" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1922734728" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1922734728" />

<div id="review_3406611577" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3406611577" />
    <a title="Théodore " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/98232817-th-odore"><img alt="Théodore " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595517509p2/98232817.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3406611577?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Théodore " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Théodore " href="/user/show/98232817-th-odore">Théodore </a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9786071103161" href="/book/show/7086348-ca-n">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3406611577" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15933088535450060628">Asking questions,and drawing attention to inconsistencies in sacred texts, is not exactly compatible with a good believer. Fortunately, a writer does not have to folow such rules. At will, he can take any character from the Bible, and put it in all possible contexts. <br/> I discovered, with pleasure, that Saramago's last novel - "Cain" - does just that : he plays, irreverently, with well- known characters from the Bible, and dismantles all conventions, paving the way for many questions concerning ma</span>
  <span id="freeText15933088535450060628" style="display:none">Asking questions,and drawing attention to inconsistencies in sacred texts, is not exactly compatible with a good believer. Fortunately, a writer does not have to folow such rules. At will, he can take any character from the Bible, and put it in all possible contexts. <br /> I discovered, with pleasure, that Saramago's last novel - "Cain" - does just that : he plays, irreverently, with well- known characters from the Bible, and dismantles all conventions, paving the way for many questions concerning man's relationship with divinity.<br /><br />An incomprehensible and unjust Cain, and a tyrannical and capricious God ( yes, you read that right ) - constantly subject to mistakes, that's exactly how I imagined the relationship between the two. But in Saramago's , Cain - the one pushed to murder because of a mocking deity, makes a pact not with the Devil, but with God : Acknowledging that he was wrong, God makes a sign on Cain's forehead, for be protected, and let him roam free. So free, that he walks where he wants, including some of the most famous stories :<br /> in the episode with Iov, with Abraham, with Lilith, and finally - in the episode with Noah's Ark.<br /><br />"Cain" - is a book written as if to revolt the wicked, with a comic and a humour doubled by the finest irony. It is, at the same time, a philosophical one, which invites an analysis that would shed a new light on some common points in the Bible. For example, the much- promoted goodness of God, which - on simple analysis, we see to be at least questionable. <br /> Saramago displays a note of astonishment not so much at the commandments of God, as at the obedience of man. The whole episode slides into a pantagruelic irony . Cain's discussions with different characters aim to demonstrate an idea, in principle the same : the hypocrisy and wickedness of the Creator are more terrible than any human weakness, his great flow being, above all - envy, and selfishness surpasses even his intelligence :<br /><br />" Instead of being proud of the sons he has, he prefers envy, it is clear that God cannot bear to see anyone happy " - a perfectly explicable situation, as long as " the history of men is the history of their misunderstandings with God, neither does he understand us , nor do we understand him ".<br /><br />Saramago has no shyness in saying things by name, the episode with Iov leading to the harshest considerations in this book. <br /> The coup de grâce is given by Cain to God, when he ascends Noah's Ark, and kills, one by one, all those who were to be the first humans, again.<br /> "Cain" - is a kind of testament of the author, is a book that invites a critical return on some points that are delivered to us, without right of replay. I'm sure there will be a lot of controversy over this book, but I hope that a voice like Saramago's will be greeted with the openness it deserves.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15933088535450060628" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3163128243" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3163128243" />
    <a title="Bill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/106293767-bill"><img alt="Bill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1576529794p2/106293767.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3163128243?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill" href="/user/show/106293767-bill">Bill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780547419893" href="/book/show/10086782-cain">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106293767-bill?shelf=nobel">nobel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106293767-bill?shelf=portugal">portugal</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106293767-bill?shelf=4-star">4-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106293767-bill?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106293767-bill?shelf=read-in-2020">read-in-2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3163128243" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10452110119926998106">By all accounts, Portuguese Nobel Prize-winner Saramago was no fan of God, although he seems to have remained God-haunted throughout his life. In this, his posthumously-published, final novel, his protagonist Cain protests the unjust violence he observes God committing as he participates in a series of reimagined stories from the Old Testament, e.g. Abraham and Isaac, the Tower of Babel, Sodom and Gomorrah, and Noah and his Ark.<br/><br/>Sentenced by God to be a "fugitive and a vagabond on earth" after mu</span>
  <span id="freeText10452110119926998106" style="display:none">By all accounts, Portuguese Nobel Prize-winner Saramago was no fan of God, although he seems to have remained God-haunted throughout his life. In this, his posthumously-published, final novel, his protagonist Cain protests the unjust violence he observes God committing as he participates in a series of reimagined stories from the Old Testament, e.g. Abraham and Isaac, the Tower of Babel, Sodom and Gomorrah, and Noah and his Ark.<br /><br />Sentenced by God to be a "fugitive and a vagabond on earth" after murdering his brother Abel, Cain not only travels through biblical space, on a donkey Saramago calls a "retired rocinante", evoking Don Quixote, but also back and forth through time. He is aware that he is experiencing these events out of sequence and his time travel plays a role in the surprise ending. Although I don't think the following quote from the book's unnamed narrator conveys the essence of Saramago's own beliefs, I found it thought-provoking:<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>The history of mankind is the history of our misunderstandings with god, for he doesn't understand us, and we don't understand him.</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="10452110119926998106" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2193313122" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2193313122" />
    <a title="Edward" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12933866-edward"><img alt="Edward" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444136022p2/12933866.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2193313122?book_show_action=true">Jan 14, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward" href="/user/show/12933866-edward">Edward</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099552246" href="/book/show/13170570-cain">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=nobel-winners">nobel-winners</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2193313122" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17912615070683688911">Compared to <i>The Gospel According to Jesus Christ</i>, Saramago is a lot more laid back here, a lot less subtle, like he's shooting fish in a barrel. But as far as contradictions and inconsistencies go, <i>The Old Testament</i> (or as Saramago calls it, <i>The Book of Nonsense</i>) is pretty low-hanging fruit. Whether it's God's destruction of innocent children in Sodom and Gomorrah, while choosing to save only the "honourable" Lot (who had earlier offered his daughters' virginity to the mob, and who later sleeps </span>
  <span id="freeText17912615070683688911" style="display:none">Compared to <i>The Gospel According to Jesus Christ</i>, Saramago is a lot more laid back here, a lot less subtle, like he's shooting fish in a barrel. But as far as contradictions and inconsistencies go, <i>The Old Testament</i> (or as Saramago calls it, <i>The Book of Nonsense</i>) is pretty low-hanging fruit. Whether it's God's destruction of innocent children in Sodom and Gomorrah, while choosing to save only the "honourable" Lot (who had earlier offered his daughters' virginity to the mob, and who later sleeps with them both himself), or God's punking of Abraham by calling for the mock-sacrifice of his son - the stories of the old testament show God to be not exactly the paragon of maturity and virtue. Saramago's take-down in <i>Cain</i> does not teach us anything new, but his approach is original and incisive, and above all it is a lot of fun.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17912615070683688911" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer370462270" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10719674055296067686">I've just read some pages, and I surely can envision much to come, of the <i>same</i>. <br/><br/>It starts quoting Hebrews 11:4. “By faith, Abel offered God a sacrifice….and by faith Abel, though dead, still speaks". A quote from the Book of "rubbish/non-sense", according to Saramago. <br/><br/>An <i>imperfect</i> Creation, that's the main issue: (1) God got aware later that Adam and Eve (in the book: adam and eve) didn’t speak: so God made a tongue (both the muscle and the idiom); afterwards the creation. (2) God corrected late</span>
  <span id="freeText10719674055296067686" style="display:none">I've just read some pages, and I surely can envision much to come, of the <i>same</i>. <br><br>It starts quoting Hebrews 11:4. “By faith, Abel offered God a sacrifice….and by faith Abel, though dead, still speaks". A quote from the Book of "rubbish/non-sense", according to Saramago. <br><br>An <i>imperfect</i> Creation, that's the main issue: (1) God got aware later that Adam and Eve (in the book: adam and eve) didn’t speak: so God made a tongue (both the muscle and the idiom); afterwards the creation. (2) God corrected later a lack of belly button; yes, for procreation purposes. He sparsely visited the couple, because he had other paradises to visit. <br><br><a href="http://imgur.com/Tq1cH" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380384296i/742784.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a> <br><br>God <i>expells</i> them from paradise after they had disobeyed. Eve said ”a serpent fooled me” in a dream. She ate from the Tree of Life; Adam too. <br><b><br><br>So my present thesis is: this is truly a manual on how to mock God*.</b><br><br>Yes, I will continue reading, to unravel the Cain issue. <br><br>»«<br><br>Adam and Eve now live in a cave…nearby four rivers. <br><br>Adam and Eve are hungry. Eve volunteers to get fruit from paradise garden. She meets with the Cherubim Azael**: asks him some fruit; he’s reluctant but then will provide her with some fruits.<br><br>Later, Adam and Eve would meet with the cherubim: he tells them: you’re not alone. Soon they will get a ride from a caravan.<br><br>Adam and Eve are portrayed as being of black hair and dark skin; but their children, Abel and Cain, are different. <i>Abel being blond, Saramago hints at the rendezvous between Eve and the angel of Eden. <br></i> <br><br>Abel and Cain have different vocations. They offer a sacrifice to God. God is pleased with Abel, but <i>despises</i> Cain’s. Abel mocks Cain; Cain kills Abel. Cain wanted to kill God. God will <i>punish</i> Cain: he’s condemned to become a wanderer…and gets a mark in his forehead. <u>The Cain sign.</u><br><br><br><a href="http://imgur.com/yrfNi" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380384296i/742785.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a> <br><br><br>»»««<br><br>“O diabo que te assinalou algum defeito encontrou”.<br><br>Cain gets to Nod land. He doesn’t say his name is Cain; he says he’s Abel (!!). He manages to get two jobs: one as clay worker and another at the palace of the governess of Nod: Lilith, the wife of Noah. Noah is jealous about that <i>second job.</i>. Noah’s men try to kill Cain, but due to his sign they aren’t succeeded. The above mentioned palace was not like Mafra or Versailles... or Buckingham's. Certainly not.<br><br>»»»«««<br><br>"O senhor não é pessoa em que se possa confiar".<br><br>Now Cain tells Lilith his true name. She asks him to kill Noah. He refuses. Lilith is pregnant. Cain departures to a new, green land. There he meets with Abraham, about to sacrifice his son Isaac to God. <u>Not the angel..., but Cain saves the boy! </u> Here I laughed. I am still laughing: the angel got late, due to a mechanical problem... in the wing (!!!). So Cain got the laurels.<br>Haha.<br>...<br>... Hahaha.<br><br><br><a href="http://imgur.com/58hXG" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380384296i/742786._SY540_.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><br>»»»»««««<br><br>Riding a donkey Cain arrives to the place where the Babel Tower was being built. But the people are in total disagreement: they speak different languages,...they looked crazy. Cain asks a man who speaks Hebrew like him: how come? The man replied: they all spoke the "Tongue", the same for everybody...but then God visited them, and didn't like the Tower. So,<i>he confused</i> the languages; <i>God was jealous</i> of men.(!!!!) <br>ha!! Cain reveals that he's got a special (supernatural?) ability: he can travel in time: back and forward.<br> <br><a href="http://imgur.com/h284p" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380384296i/742787.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a> <br><br>»»»»»«««««<br><br>Allow me to abbreviate.<br><br>Cain reports on Sodom and Gomorra: fire and sulfur rain falls upon the cities…. Lot’s wife turning into a statue of salt. The same <i>merciless God</i> who didn’t save the children. <br><br>Moses 40 days and 40 nights on the Sinai;… now Cain says he’s Noah. God gets his <i>revenge</i> on people for their adoration of the golden calf. Cain leaves because he’s sick about bloodshed.<br><br>Joshua and Jericho’s walls that fell after the sound of the trumpets… Cain says the stopping of the sun is a <i>lie</i>.<br><br><a href="http://imgur.com/OrR3g" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380384296i/742788._SX540_.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a> <br><br>Noah is building the Arc because God wants to <i>exterminate men</i> ,…in a land full of inequities. Cain joins Noah’s family….but exposes <i>God's ignorance</i> on the Archimedes principle.(!!!)<br><br>The flood. 40 days and nights of rain. After 150 days, the water level starts receding. Noah had seen an unicorn. Really, all animals in the ark? The ark had no sail... nor engine.<br>By the end of the book all humans are dead...but Cain. Noah committed suicide. <br><br> <br><br><b>Saramago took pity on Cain. ”Despite the murder, Cain is an intrinsically honest man…with moral sense”. I would say Cain looks <s>godlike</s> <i>godish</i>. His forehead sign is becoming bigger. Like a <b>black sun</b>.</b><br><br><a href="http://imgur.com/R3zSBog" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1437756430i/15625929.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><a href="http://imgur.com/mYTzXdx" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1437756095i/15625901._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><br>* Saramago interviewed by Spanish EFE. He was 86 years-old and he said: “Dios no es una persona de fiar…no estoy questionando Dios, …la humanidad que lo ha inventado”. Which means "God is not a trustworthy person...I am not questioning God...but mankind that has invented God".<br><br>**Jewish legend speaks of Azazel as the angel who refused to bow down to Adam. For such refusal, Azazel was thenceforth dubbed "the accursed Satan".....Azazel refused, contending "why should a son of fire (an angel) fall down before a son of clay (a mortal)". Whereupon God cast Azazel out of Heaven.... Milton in PARADISE LOST I, 534 describes Azazel as "a cherub tall" but also as a fallen angel...<br><br>in: A Dictionary of Angels by Gustav Davidson</span>
