<div id="review_2404520037" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2404520037" />
    <a title="Fede" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/73964263-fede"><img alt="Fede" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604333859p2/73964263.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2404520037?book_show_action=true">May 27, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fede" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fede" href="/user/show/73964263-fede">Fede</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099552246" href="/book/show/13170570-cain">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73964263-fede?shelf=spanish-portuguese-south-american">spanish-portuguese-south-american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73964263-fede?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2404520037" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3419073149977612036">Eighteen years after "<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28859.The_Gospel_According_to_Jesus_Christ' title='The Gospel According to Jesus Christ by José Saramago' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Gospel According to Jesus Christ</a>", his merciless onslaught against the New Testament, Saramago is back to finish the job. The result is an equally outrageous and - alas - good job, a crazy retelling of the Old Testament as seen through the eyes of the most controversial character of all: Cain, the first murder in human history, the Evil Brother who is actually the Evil Half of mankind... of any single human being, that is. <br/><br/><br/>The book of Genesis is full of murders, despair</span>
  <span id="freeText3419073149977612036" style="display:none">Eighteen years after "<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28859.The_Gospel_According_to_Jesus_Christ" title="The Gospel According to Jesus Christ by José Saramago" rel="nofollow noopener">The Gospel According to Jesus Christ</a>", his merciless onslaught against the New Testament, Saramago is back to finish the job. The result is an equally outrageous and - alas - good job, a crazy retelling of the Old Testament as seen through the eyes of the most controversial character of all: Cain, the first murder in human history, the Evil Brother who is actually the Evil Half of mankind... of any single human being, that is. <br /><br /><br />The book of Genesis is full of murders, despair, deviant sex, fear. Quite properly, one might say, since it tells about our common origins; its protagonists are mostly sinful creatures whose identities are to be seen as the chain of mankind's moral development. A few examples:<br />Adam: "Man", "Human being".<br />Abel: possibly deriving from Havel or Hevel, "Breath", "Wind", "Vanity" (referring to the shortness of Abel's life). <br />Cain: "Possession"...<br />...and so on. <br />The whole Testament is a collection of extreme contradictions: creation and destruction, ferocity and mercy, lust and purity. All the insoluble questions and indecipherable answers of theology are to be found in these books. No wonder Saramago finds an easy target in so many pages of rape, incest, massacres, revenge, extermination and - most of all - in a God presiding over any sort of evil afflicting His creatures.<br /><br /><br />After having murdered his brother, Saramago's Cain is condemned to wander through time and space until he dies. He finds himself riding a donkey in a barren wasteland, sort of a metaphysical desert in which humanity dwells in a surreal atmosphere of fleetingness. There's neither a past nor a future for him: only a continuously shifting present. That's how he witnesses the main episodes of Genesis, Exodus, Hosea, Job, obviously revisited by Saramago in the most scandalous way possible, with no chronological order so to describe the nonsensical life of mankind. Once again, the author's point is quite simple to get: God is cruel, selfish and utterly indifferent both to good and evil; we are puppets whose existence has always been meaningless to the great puppeteer. <br /><br /><br />"God, the creator of heaven and earth, is completely insane."<br /><br /><br />To start with, God is as guilty as Cain since He didn't prevent him from killing his brother. God agrees with Cain's accusations and spares his life, so to get even with him; then our hero sets off for his lifelong journey through the Unknown. <br />He meets Lilith (not the female demon of the Semitic myth), the sensual, gorgeous wife of a landowner who becomes his mistress and bears him a child, Enoch, "Founder of cities" (an interesting parallel can be drawn with the relationship between Kamala and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/52036.Siddhartha" title="Siddhartha by Hermann Hesse" rel="nofollow noopener">Siddhartha</a> in Hesse's novel). <br />Then Cain saves Isaac from being sacrificed by his father Abraham, who's being tested by God. The angel sent to stop the slaughtering arrives too late and only Cain's fortuitous arrival averts the tragedy:<br /><br /><br />"Isaac asked, Then the lord is capable of anything, the good, the bad and the worst (...) Father, this religion is not my thing.<br />It must be, son, there is no remedy. <br />You mean the lord makes people go insane, Isaac asked.<br />Yes, quite often, most of the time, abraham replied."<br /><br /><br />He also sees the tower of Babel destroyed by God's rage; thus He punishes the human attempt to reach the sky, a pious dream of spiritual elevation He reciprocates with terror and mayhem. More bloodshed in Sodom, where the Lord doesn't even bother discerning between the guilty and the innocent and exterminates the whole population, including the children... who can't reasonably have committed that sort of sin yet, Cain points out. <br />On with the conquest of the Promised Land and the trail of blood left behind by Joshua's army; the Golden Calf; the destruction of Jericho... <br />Then Cain meets Noah and his family, chosen by God to start a brand new generation after the Deluge. The last human beings welcome him on board the Ark, where they're supposed to spend their time copulating for the sake of mankind. Cain's plan is quite different, though: in fact he doesn't waste the chance to take his revenge against a God he holds responsible of all the horrors he's witnessed along his journey... <br /><br /><br />Saramago's writing is - as always - characterized by long convoluted sentences, with no direct speech and huge paragraphs; in this case he also does away with capitalizing the characters' names: god, adam, cain, abraham, as though they were merely conceptual entities rather than beings, which is not exactly a heresy, to a certain extent (see the above-mentioned examples). <br /><br /><br />This novel is much shorter than "<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28859.The_Gospel_According_to_Jesus_Christ" title="The Gospel According to Jesus Christ by José Saramago" rel="nofollow noopener">The Gospel According to Jesus Christ</a>", but it's nonetheless equally disturbing and thought-provoking, a terribly serious satire of... <br />Of what? <br />Religion? Atheism?<br />Both? <br />Saramago's theological background is neither rigourous nor creative; and yet he's able to trigger the most unexpected reflections and raise lots of tricky questions.<br />His attacks are the same we bigoted papists have been dealing with for two thousand years; I dare say atheism has lost much of its intellectual strenght nowadays, and it's certainly not in Saramago's work that any real answer is to be found. <br />And still, there's something I can't help but like in his blasphemous books, something that makes it impossible for me to dismiss them as literary sensationalism. They're sort of a strange challenge against myself, an amazing experience in which such a cold-blooded reader as I am enjoyed reading for the sake of it - despite the meaning, or the lack of it. <br />I suppose it's one of those cases in which the artistic value prevails over any other consideration.<br /><br /><br />(The word "brotherhood" is, to be sure, a fine word, but we oughtn't to forget its ambiguity. The first pair of brothers in the history of the world were, according to the Bible, Cain and Abel, and the one murdered the other.)<br />― Pope Benedict XVI</span>
  <a data-text-id="3419073149977612036" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1475063151?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ray" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ray" href="/user/show/4908095-ray">Ray</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099552246" href="/book/show/13170570-cain">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4908095-ray?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1475063151" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10088314494871575503">Cain kills his brother Abel in a fit of jealousy and is forced to wander the earth in penance. This book shows us what Cain did next. We see him pass through many of the key events of the Old Testament. He is there for example at the testing of Job, the razing of the walls of Jericho and the destruction of Sodom and Gomorrah.<br/><br/>Throughout he is in dialogue with God. He understands not how the God of love and justice can be capricious, spiteful, jealous and uncaring - or as Tim Minchin memorably put</span>
  <span id="freeText10088314494871575503" style="display:none">Cain kills his brother Abel in a fit of jealousy and is forced to wander the earth in penance. This book shows us what Cain did next. We see him pass through many of the key events of the Old Testament. He is there for example at the testing of Job, the razing of the walls of Jericho and the destruction of Sodom and Gomorrah.<br /><br />Throughout he is in dialogue with God. He understands not how the God of love and justice can be capricious, spiteful, jealous and uncaring - or as Tim Minchin memorably put it "a sexist, racist, murderous c*nt"<br /><br />In this book Saramago is playful, mischievous and wise as he toys with the contradictions posed by God as portrayed in the OT. I liked the gentle humour, it reminded me a little of Vonnegut at his best, and for me there can be no greater accolade.<br /><br />Worth a read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10088314494871575503" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2171446044" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2171446044" />
    <a title="Vivian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3947172-vivian"><img alt="Vivian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1458107851p2/3947172.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2171446044?book_show_action=true">Nov 06, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vivian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vivian" href="/user/show/3947172-vivian">Vivian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780547419893" href="/book/show/10086782-cain">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3947172-vivian?shelf=2017-odyssey">2017-odyssey</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3947172-vivian?shelf=library">library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3947172-vivian?shelf=humor">humor</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2171446044" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15582863006015368308"><b>
  <blockquote>"Blessed are those who choose sedition because theirs is the kingdom of Earth."</blockquote>
</b><br/><br/>CAIN is to the Old Testament as DECAMERON is to 14th century (Catholic) Church. <br/><br/>One could argue, quite easily that it is blasphemous. Cain is the protagonist in this time traveling jaunt through the highlights of 'What was God thinking?' in the Old Testament. <br/><br/>This is small taste as Cain witnesses a discussion between Abraham and Isaac:<br/><b>
  <blockquote>"Father, I don't understand this religion, But you have to, my son you have n</blockquote></b></span>
  <span id="freeText15582863006015368308" style="display:none"><b>
  <blockquote>"Blessed are those who choose sedition because theirs is the kingdom of Earth."</blockquote>
</b><br><br>CAIN is to the Old Testament as DECAMERON is to 14th century (Catholic) Church. <br><br>One could argue, quite easily that it is blasphemous. Cain is the protagonist in this time traveling jaunt through the highlights of 'What was God thinking?' in the Old Testament. <br><br>This is small taste as Cain witnesses a discussion between Abraham and Isaac:<br><b>
  <blockquote>"Father, I don't understand this religion, But you have to, my son you have no choice, and now I must make a request, a humble request, What is it, Let us forget what happened here, Well, I am not sure I can, father, I can still see myself bound, lying on top of the pyre, and your arm raised, the blade of the knife glinting, That wasn't me, I would never do such a thing in my right mind, Do you mean that the lord makes people mad, asked isaac, Yes, he often does, almost always, replied abraham, Even if that were true, you were still the one with the knife in your hand, [...]</blockquote>
</b><br><br>I transcribed this exactly as presented in my ebook copy, including lack of full stops which is a little tricky with the dialogue shifts since the only clue is first letter capitalization. The lack of proper name capitalization was less disconcerting. That said, the formatting made this more difficult. I don't know if the print version has a different layout so it resembles verse instead of prose, but as this is, my eyes had a hard time tracking and skipped lines which necessitated rereading. It was my least favorite part of the book.<br><br>This book is basically a humorous version of the thoughts that go through my mind when faced with the Bible. <br><b>
  <blockquote>"The road to self-deception is narrow to begin with, but there is always someone to broaden it out, for as the proverb says, self-deception is like eating or scratching, it's all a matter of beginning."</blockquote>
</b><br>And for any persons feeling the need to tell me I'm going to hell, don't bother.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1509972357i/24374807._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I feel like I've made progress. Usually when I take these quizzes, I end up in level 7 with the warmongers and suicidals drowning in a river of blood.<br><br>Buzzfeed Quiz: <a href="https://www.buzzfeed.com/juliapugachevsky/which-circle-of-hell-will-you-go-to?utm_term=.myeBA7wl9#.vswpDwRr2" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>Which Circle of Hell are You Going to?</b>
</a><br><br><b><u>Favorite quote:</u> <br><blockquote>According to the ancients, the devil's wiles would never prevail over the will of god, but I'm not sure now that things are that simple, it seems likely that satan is just another instrument of lord, the one who does the dirty work to which god prefers not to put his name.</blockquote></b></span>
  <a data-text-id="15582863006015368308" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_208144606" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/208144606" />
    <a title="switterbug (Betsey)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2843912-switterbug-betsey"><img alt="switterbug (Betsey)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374315754p2/2843912.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/208144606?book_show_action=true">Sep 13, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="switterbug (Betsey)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="switterbug (Betsey)" href="/user/show/2843912-switterbug-betsey">switterbug (Betsey)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780547419893" href="/book/show/10086782-cain">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer208144606" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14889603903708128303">God is ineffable, say his disciples. In Saramago's recreation of the old testament, god is snide, foppish, vicious, capricious, puerile, contemptuous, crabby, and a slouch at multi-tasking (and no iconic capital letters for this merry band of pranksters). This short but adventure-packed novella presents a new twist on the story of cain, the fratricidal brother. Weed out all the boring parts of Genesis, feature all the greatest hits, and place cain as the ubiquitous character. Actually, cain even</span>
  <span id="freeText14889603903708128303" style="display:none">God is ineffable, say his disciples. In Saramago's recreation of the old testament, god is snide, foppish, vicious, capricious, puerile, contemptuous, crabby, and a slouch at multi-tasking (and no iconic capital letters for this merry band of pranksters). This short but adventure-packed novella presents a new twist on the story of cain, the fratricidal brother. Weed out all the boring parts of Genesis, feature all the greatest hits, and place cain as the ubiquitous character. Actually, cain even shows up at events that god is too distracted to attend (and his angels are stuck in traffic?).<br /><br />In addition to being an erudite little gem, this story is full of slicing irony mixed with slapstick humor and dry, bone-dry, desert-dry wit. He's too subtle to just rant on the lord. Saramago seems to be peeking out of the corners of the pages, winking at the reader, offering a sly smile. As an arch example, Eve calls herself the First Lady of Paradise.<br /><br />Saramago, who won the Nobel Prize for Literature in 1998, was an ardent atheist, who said that the Bible is a handbook of bad morals. He also impugns a "cruel, jealous, and inhumane God (who) exists only in our heads." In 1992, a scandal erupted after The Gospel According to Jesus Christ was published, whose characterization of God didn't conform to certain pieties, so Saramago happily moved to the Spanish Canary Islands, where he lived until his death in 2010. He didn't think CAIN would offend Catholics, though, "because they don't read the Bible."<br /><br />This is his final contribution to the world of literature. Although not his most superlative--by definition it is contained--it is still masterful, and had me laughing with glee at intervals. Check out his 1995 Blindness for a novel of unsurpassed beauty, staggering tragedy, and stunning redemption.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14889603903708128303" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_965949909" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/965949909" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16077597-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582900704p2/16077597.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/965949909?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/16077597-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19362567-kain">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16077597-matt?shelf=media-ebooks">media-ebooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16077597-matt?shelf=eyes-on-the-prize">eyes-on-the-prize</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer965949909" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10395848597092101407">This is the last work of José Saramago. The older he got, the more freely he felt, and the more radically he took a political stand, and throughout his life the author has made no secret of being an atheist. The publication of this book has led to some confusion and heated debates, and Saramago was called a "heretic" by the Portuguese bishops.<br/><br/>The book tells the story of Cain being cast by God to the land of Nod, <em>East of Eden</em> to lead a miserably life. From there Cain takes off to some kind of tim</span>
  <span id="freeText10395848597092101407" style="display:none">This is the last work of José Saramago. The older he got, the more freely he felt, and the more radically he took a political stand, and throughout his life the author has made no secret of being an atheist. The publication of this book has led to some confusion and heated debates, and Saramago was called a "heretic" by the Portuguese bishops.<br><br>The book tells the story of Cain being cast by God to the land of Nod, <em>East of Eden</em> to lead a miserably life. From there Cain takes off to some kind of time travel and visits various places and people mentioned in the Old Testament of the Bible: Adam and Eve, Abraham and Isaac, Moses, Noah; Sodom and Gomorrah, Sinai, Jericho, ...<br><br>God also plays a role, and not a particularly positive one. Cain (the fictitious Cain, that is Saramago's Cain, who is based on the (also fictitious?) Cain of the Bible) considers God a hypocrite, who cannot be trusted, even calls him a SOB and would rather send him to hell. Strong words! You could call this story some kind of <em>Anti-Fanfic</em> of the bible. Moreover, it is quite brutal and sexy in some places, but this is true for the Old Testament as well.<br><br>I'm not very well-versed in the Bible, so I probably missed out on a few topics. The rest I found rather enjoyable and cracked a few smiles. I cannot easily be dumbfounded. If you're a religious person though, I think you should better stay away from this book.<br><br><a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-sa/3.0/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img alt="Creative Commons License" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380148217i/680962.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br>This work is licensed under a <a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-sa/3.0/" rel="nofollow noopener">Creative Commons Attribution-NonCommercial-ShareAlike 3.0 Unported License</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10395848597092101407" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        rated it

<span id="freeTextContainer16035399619841116230">Exactly my cup of tea. Wish it was longer though, that is the main complaint I have about this book. <br/>The writing style was a little odd at the beginning, but I got used to it pretty quickly (to the weird format of dialogue in the book, especially). <br/><br/><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“our god, the creator of heaven and earth, is completely mad”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br/><br/>I think that this quote summarizes the book perfectly. If you're in a mood for reading slightly blasphemous, but yet funny and thought-provoking book with just above 150 pages, l</span>
  <span id="freeText16035399619841116230" style="display:none">Exactly my cup of tea. Wish it was longer though, that is the main complaint I have about this book. <br />The writing style was a little odd at the beginning, but I got used to it pretty quickly (to the weird format of dialogue in the book, especially). <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“our god, the creator of heaven and earth, is completely mad”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br /><br />I think that this quote summarizes the book perfectly. If you're in a mood for reading slightly blasphemous, but yet funny and thought-provoking book with just above 150 pages, look no further. <br />I enjoyed the Old Testament stories and was thinking ahead most of the time, trying to guess which one will be next. Also, I love how our main character, one of the people that are described as pure evil throughout our history, isn't what we all heard he is. He is the Cain from the story, of course. And he did kill his brother. But we get to see his way of thinking, and his way of trying to explain everything that occurs in this short novel, and I think that the perception of good and evil gets a little bit shifted by the end of the book.<br /><br />All in all, it was way better than I expected it to be, and I really enjoyed reading it. I think it is suited for everyone and that every single person can get something out of this book and understand it in their own way.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>
    <b>“Oh, I doubt that, we gods are like bottomless wells, if you lean over us, you won't even see your image reflected back” <br /></b>
  </i>
</blockquote></span>

<div id="review_216758389" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/216758389" />
    <a title="Lakis Fourouklas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6312888-lakis-fourouklas"><img alt="Lakis Fourouklas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442616092p2/6312888.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/216758389?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lakis Fourouklas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lakis Fourouklas" href="/user/show/6312888-lakis-fourouklas">Lakis Fourouklas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780547419893" href="/book/show/10086782-cain">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer216758389" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12375306204345689120">“The history of mankind is the history of our misunderstandings with god, for he doesn’t understand us and we don’t understand him.”<br/> This is one of those books that one truly enjoys to read; and I did. Saramago in this, his last offering before he passed away, recreates in his own special way the life of Cain and offers the reader, especially the one who’s not too attached to the words of the scriptures, a chance to have a few good laughs. While reading it, I dare to admit that, I found myself a</span>
  <span id="freeText12375306204345689120" style="display:none">“The history of mankind is the history of our misunderstandings with god, for he doesn’t understand us and we don’t understand him.”<br /> This is one of those books that one truly enjoys to read; and I did. Saramago in this, his last offering before he passed away, recreates in his own special way the life of Cain and offers the reader, especially the one who’s not too attached to the words of the scriptures, a chance to have a few good laughs. While reading it, I dare to admit that, I found myself agreeing time and again with the somewhat heretical views of the author.<br /> At the beginning it was not to the Word, but simply the creation of the world, since, as we read, when god made man forgot to give to him the gift of speech. However, after he did give it to him maybe he came to regret it because if he hadn’t then Cain wouldn’t be able to speak and thus verbally annoy and abuse him for all eternity.<br /> First things first though. According to Saramago the first people were less than perfect. They were kind of stupid and full of flaws. When they were expelled from paradise actually “Adam and Eve resembled a couple of orangutans who had stood upright for the first time.” However, as time started going by they improved a bit, since they now had to work to make a living. As the story goes they set up home on the banks of the Tigris and Euphrates rivers, where with the help of the animals that followed them to exile, they started working the land. Of course at the beginning things were not so easy for them so Eve, every now and then, had to take a walk towards paradise and beg the angels to give her some fruits. However that didn’t last for too long since sooner rather than later they started becoming self-sufficient and independent until someday, without quite understanding how or why, they even became parents. Abel and Cain are their famous kids, but of course they had many more. Abel, the innocent one according to the official story, was in reality an infuriating character, somebody who found joy in provoking and insulting the others and who always thought that he was the leader of the pack. He really was scum, thus Cain, who could suffer his behavior no longer, ended up killing him. And that’s exactly how his long skirmish with god has begun. And that’s exactly when his long journey started; a journey that would carry him back and forth in time, a kind of a road trip into the Old and the New Testament, full of surprises and with many not so light touches of humor.<br /> Cain, in this book, is portrayed as the first hero in Hebrew history; someone who’s extremely daring and not in the least afraid of death, and who doesn’t miss a chance to give god a piece of his mind. In order to do that he travels a lot, so we meet him in Sodom and Gomorrah, at the Sacrifice of Abraham, on Noah’s Ark, at The Tower of Babel, in the desert visiting Moses’ camp, while he drops by in the New Testament as well to act the role of the Good Samaritan. Wherever he goes he uses the name of Abel, and at every single place he encounters the ugly face of god; a god that he’s determined to oppose in every way. He’s not your normal kind of hero, he seeks no redemption, but instead he asks of god to apologize to him for mistreating him.<br /> This is not one of those books that I would recommend to everyone because of its subject matter. However I’m sure that those who are familiar with the author’s work, as well as with the writings of Norman Mailer, Robert Graves and Nikos Kazantzakis, will truly enjoy reading it. Saramago doesn’t only seem to want to provoke the reader, but also to say to him that just believing something without questioning it, is the easy path; the one that leads to ignorance and cheats him of his free will and independent mind.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12375306204345689120" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_217598695" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/217598695" />
    <a title="jeremy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/635936-jeremy"><img alt="jeremy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569705896p2/635936.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/217598695?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="jeremy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="jeremy" href="/user/show/635936-jeremy">jeremy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780547419893" href="/book/show/10086782-cain">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/635936-jeremy?shelf=translation">translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/635936-jeremy?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer217598695" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17328805601145764845">the late josé saramago was quite the prolific writer, having composed over thirty books, including an array of acclaimed novels, poetry, and journals. actively writing until his death at the age of 87 in 2010, the portuguese nobel laureate's international renown was often marked by controversy and criticism. <i>cain (caim)</i>, his final work, was published in his native language in 2009, and stirred many of the same sentiments as his earlier, thematically-linked novel, <i>the gospel according to jesus ch</i></span>
  <span id="freeText17328805601145764845" style="display:none">the late josé saramago was quite the prolific writer, having composed over thirty books, including an array of acclaimed novels, poetry, and journals. actively writing until his death at the age of 87 in 2010, the portuguese nobel laureate's international renown was often marked by controversy and criticism. <i>cain (caim)</i>, his final work, was published in his native language in 2009, and stirred many of the same sentiments as his earlier, thematically-linked novel, <i>the gospel according to jesus christ</i>. <br /><br /><i>cain</i> (with yet another enviable rendering by longtime translator margaret jull costa), begins in the garden of eden, wherein god realizes he had neglected to endow adam and eve with the gift of speech. shortly thereafter, saramago introduces cain, and the well-known tale of his fratricidal encounter with abel. when called to account for his actions by god, cain indicts god for his own culpability in the murder. cain is marked and exiled, and left to wander his many years. <br /><br />throughout the novel, saramago reimagines much of the hebrew bible, allowing cain to appear in many of the most famous old testament sites and stories (including the tower of babel, sodom and gomorrah, jericho, and the tales of abraham and isaac, noah and ham, joshua, and job). cain's presence and interactions with the aforementioned characters is most often as witness, an opportunity he takes to call out god for the needless suffering and cruelty he inflicts on others (including innocent bystanders). the choice of incorporating the most violent and tumultuous biblical episodes into the narrative was, of course, intentional, as one of saramago's aims was to highlight the vindictive, jealous, depraved, and incorrigible behavior of the judeo-christian god. <br /><br />colored by saramago's trademark prose, <i>cain</i> is a brief, yet often funny novel that will serve as his final, passing denunciation of organized religion. to many, <i>cain</i> will be not only objectionable, but also heretical. upon its publication in portugal, saramago remarked that while his book had offended the church, it wasn't likely to offend catholics, as "they don't read the bible." he went on to characterize the bible as "a manual of bad morals," a "catalogue of cruelties and of the worst of human nature." saramago himself described <i>cain</i> as "an insurrection, an exhortation for everyone to dare to look for what is on the other side of things."<br /><br />saramago's outspoken nature and commitment to a variety of political, international, and human rights issues was well known, one he felt compelled to not as artist or author, but as engaged global citizen. to be sure, many of his novels are books of ideas, parables, or deeply conceived allegories. while his writing was always fluid and graceful, his opinions were often anything but subtle. <i>cain</i>, while not his most accomplished work, will undoubtedly be seen as an important piece within his distinguished oeuvre. that he was able to continue to craft such vigorous and unequivocal fiction well into his mid-80s is, in and of itself, quite remarkable. josé saramago ought to be remembered as one of the all-time greats of world literature, and whatever praise his writing continues to attract well into future decades will be rightfully befitting.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>like everything else, words have their why and wherefores. some call to us solemnly, arrogantly, giving themselves airs, as if they were destined for great things, and then it turns out that they were nothing more than a breeze too light even to set the sail of a windmill moving, whereas other ordinary, habitual words, the sort you use every day, end up having consequences no one would have dared predict, they weren't born for that and yet they shook the world.</i>
</blockquote><br /><blockquote>
  <i>the history of mankind is the history of our misunderstandings with god, for he doesn't understand us, and we don't understand him.</i>
</blockquote><br /><blockquote>
  <i>according to the ancients, the devil's wiles would never prevail over the will of god, but i'm not sure now that things are that simple, it seems likely that satan is just another instrument of the lord, the one who does the dirty work to which god prefers not to put his name.</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="17328805601145764845" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_879586231" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/879586231" />
    <a title="Caterina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5558460-caterina"><img alt="Caterina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1487431115p2/5558460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/879586231?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Caterina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Caterina" href="/user/show/5558460-caterina">Caterina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780547419893" href="/book/show/10086782-cain">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5558460-caterina?shelf=nobel">nobel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5558460-caterina?shelf=spec-fic">spec-fic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5558460-caterina?shelf=novels-novellas">novels-novellas</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5558460-caterina?shelf=portuguese-author">portuguese-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5558460-caterina?shelf=portuguese-english-tran">portuguese-english-tran</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5558460-caterina?shelf=biblical-reinterpretation">biblical-reinterpretation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5558460-caterina?shelf=myth-mythopoeia">myth-mythopoeia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer879586231" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9330045937682679721"><i>”It’s simple enough. I killed abel because I couldn’t kill you, so, in intent, you are dead too.”</i>—cain<br/><br/>2.5/5 José Saramago is one of my favorite authors not only for his lyrical, compassionate, and humane storytelling, but because he takes aim with scathing satire at the violent and oppressive “powers that be.” I always have to grapple with his confrontational thought, it changes me. This book makes me uneasy, makes me wrestle with my angel, I both like and dislike it.<br/><br/><i>Cain</i> reimagines the biblical</span>
  <span id="freeText9330045937682679721" style="display:none"><i>”It’s simple enough. I killed abel because I couldn’t kill you, so, in intent, you are dead too.”</i>—cain<br /><br />2.5/5 José Saramago is one of my favorite authors not only for his lyrical, compassionate, and humane storytelling, but because he takes aim with scathing satire at the violent and oppressive “powers that be.” I always have to grapple with his confrontational thought, it changes me. This book makes me uneasy, makes me wrestle with my angel, I both like and dislike it.<br /><br /><i>Cain</i> reimagines the biblical stories of the creation of adam and eve, the expulsion from the garden of eden, and the fatal rivalry of cain and abel. It revisits disturbing and appalling stories from the book of genesis, where cain confronts “the lord god” on what appear to be his heedless, abusive, and murderous actions. To my mind, the biblical writers themselves seem to have started this conversation with their own embedded protest and argument. <i>Cain</i> is outright hostile and mocking to the bible, calling it “the book of nonsense.” Yet <i>Cain’s</i> reimagined, embellished stories, siding with the human beings against an unfair god, are at first compassionate, whimsical and lyrical. Saramago’s cain becomes a man who stands up to, calls out, argues with, and will not accept the unacceptable, violent, oppressive deeds he witnesses. As the book goes on, however, it becomes a bit of a tedious rant, consisting mostly of cain witnessing various violent episodes in the bible, copied verbatim, and protesting them. It’s as if Saramago felt the need to club his readers over the head, like cain who has just clubbed abel in the titian painting on the cover of my edition of this book.<br /><br />And yet — angry, jealous, vengeful, neglectful of his children (by lilith, his lover, whom he also neglects) and murderous — Cain ultimately reflects the disturbing image and likeness (according to Saramago) of his hated divine grandfather. Was that what Saramago intended?<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9330045937682679721" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('GM/PfFHhX6IL/zgrxFQrhxlidKwYyeQcZvmN3mrqysbbCDNaZowBbsVHJl55o0eayWCAQJPr0zrOCrw9kPvFRw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('UDbOj0AcSSk759PAED1Q3eynbnfYa4lbqYojwrlEEoyT8TKpd3EX5fVfzbWtyjzAPKWam1NJvn0BeRIhQ1UdDQ==')})
  }

O livro foi publicado no Porto em 1888. A acção de Os Maias passa-se em Lisboa, na segunda metade do século XIX, e apresent<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText1589957817885051557\" style=\"display:none\">Biblioteca Ulisseia de Autores Portugueses #15\n\nOs Maias é uma das obras mais conhecidas do escritor português Eça de Queiroz. O livro foi publicado no Porto em 1888. A acção de Os Maias passa-se em Lisboa, na segunda metade do século XIX, e apresenta-nos a história de três gerações da família Maia. A acção inicia-se no Outono de 1875, quando Afonso da Maia, nobre e pobre proprietário, se instala no Ramalhete com o neto recém formado em Medicina. Neste momento faz-se uma longa descrição da casa - &quot;O Ramalhete,&quot; cujo nome tem origem num painel de azulejos com um ramo de girassóis, e não em algo fresco ou campestre, tal como o nome nos remete a pensar. Afonso da Maia era o personagem mais simpático do romance e aquele que o autor mais valorizou, pois não se lhe conhecem defeitos. É um homem de carácter, culto e requintado nos gostos. Em jovem aderiu aos ideais do Liberalismo e foi obrigado, por seu pai, a sair de casa e a instalar-se em Inglaterra. Após o pai falecer regressa a Lisboa para casar com Maria Eduarda Runa, mas pouco tempo depois escolhe o exílio por razões de ordem política. Há em Os Maias um retrato da Lisboa da epóca. Carlos, que mora na Rua das Janelas Verdes, caminha com frequência até ao Rossio (embora, por vezes, vá a cavalo ou de carruagem). Algumas das lojas citadas no livro ainda existem - a Casa Havaneza, no Chiado, por exemplo. É possível seguir os diferentes percursos de Carlos ou do Ega pelas suas da Baixa lisboeta, ainda que algumas tenham mudado de nome. No final do livro, quando Carlos volta a Lisboa muitos anos depois, somos levados a ver as novidades - a Avenida da Liberdade, que substituiu o Passeio Público, e que é descrita como uma coisa nova, e feia pela sua novidade, exactamente como nos anos 70 se falava das casas de emigrante. O romance veicula sobre o país uma perspectiva muito derrotista, muito pessimista. Tirando a natureza (o Tejo, Sintra, Santa Olávia...), é tudo uma choldra ignóbil. Predomina uma visão de estrangeirado, de quem só valoriza as civilizações superiores - da França e Inglaterra, principalmente. Os políticos são mesquinhos, ignorantes ou corruptos (Gouvarinho, Sousa Neto...); os homens das Letras sao boémios e dissolutos, retrógrados ou distantes da realidade concreta (Alencar, Ega...: lembre-se o que se passou no Sarau do Teatro da Trindade); os jornalistas boémios e venais (Palma...); os homens do desporto não conseguem organizar uma corrida de cavalos, pois não há hipódromo à altura, nem cavalos, nem cavaleiros, as pessoas não vestem como o evento exigia, as senhoras traziam vestidos de missa. Para cúmulo de tudo isto, os protagonistas acabam vencidos da vida. Apesar de ser isto referido no fim do livro, pode-se ver que ainda há alguma esperanca implícita, nas passagens em que Carlos da Maia e João da Ega dizem que o apetite humano é a causa de todos os seus problemas e que portanto nunca mais terão apetites, mas logo a seguir dizem que lhes está a apetecer um &quot;prato de paio com ervilhas,&quot; ou quando dizem que a pressa não leva a nada e que a vida deve ser levada com calma mas começam a correr para apanhar o americano (eléctrico). Mais do que crítica de costumes, o romance mostra-nos um país - sobretudo Lisboa - que se dissolve, incapaz de se regenerar. Quando o autor escreve mais tarde A Cidade e as Serras, expõe uma atitude muito mais construtiva: o protagonista regenera-se pela descoberta das raízes rurais ancestrais não atingidas pela degradação da civilização, num movimento inverso ao que predomina n\'Os Maias.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"1589957817885051557\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/1285555.Jos_Saramago"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1497455560p3/1285555.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/1285555.Jos_Saramago">José Saramago
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
12,208
followers
</div>

</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8004299579810185211">José Saramago is one of the most important international writers of the last hundred years. Born in Portugal in 1922, he was in his sixties when he came to prominence as a writer with the publication of Baltasar and Blimunda. A huge body of work followed, translated into more than forty languages, and in 1998 he was awarded the Nobel Prize in Literature. Saramago died in June 2010.</span>
